The First Figure of Qiyas: Conditions and Objections
Describes the first figure of qiyas and discusses its conditions and objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for a valid first figure qiyas (universal major, affirmative actual minor) and how objections to these conditions are answered.

Core Idea
The first figure of qiyas requires a universal major, an affirmative actual minor, and yields four necessary conclusions.

In this passage, the author explains the first figure of qiyas (syllogism), which is the foundational syllogistic form. He states its conditions and then addresses objections raised against them. Understanding this figure is essential for mastering logical reasoning in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without trying to analyze. Get the overall flow of the author's argument.
The passage is dense; do not worry about details at first.
2
Identify the conditions of the first figure
Look for the conditions: universal major (kulliyya al-kubra), affirmative minor (ijab al-sughra), and the requirement of actuality (fi'liyya) according to Ibn Sina.
Notice that the author states these conditions explicitly.
3
Understand the dispute on actuality
Note the difference between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi: Ibn Sina requires the minor to be actual, while al-Farabi allows a possible minor to produce a conclusion.
This shows that there is some flexibility in the conditions.
4
Examine the objections
The text raises two objections: one about the necessity of a universal major (if only probable) and one about the necessity of an affirmative minor (since a negative premise might work).
Try to see why these objections challenge the standard conditions.
5
Review the responses
The author answers each objection: for the first, he explains that universal probability suffices; for the second, he shows that a negative minor requires an extra premise (qiyas al-musawa).
These responses defend the original conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صوره خمس: الأولى أن يعلم حكم إيجابي أو سلبي لكل أفراد شيء هو الأوسط ثم يعلم ثبوته لآخر كله أو بعضه فيعلم ثبوت ذلك الحكم للآخر كذلك قطعاً حاصلاً بالبديهة، فقد أشار إلى كلية كبرى الشكل الأول، وإيجاب صغراه مع فعليتهما وإلى نتائجه الأربع اللازمة من ضروبه بالأربعة لزوماً ضرورياً

قوله : فقد أشار إلى كلية كبرى إلخ) بقوله : لكل أفراد شيء وإيجاب صغراه مع فعليتها بقوله ثم يعلم ثبوته حيث خص الثبوت بالذكر، ثم فعلية الصغرى شرط على رأي ابن سينا، حيث اعتبر عقد الوضع بالفعل، وأما إذا اعتبر عقد الوضع بالإمكان كما هو رأي الفارابي فالصغرى الممكنة منتج في الشكل الأول، وما قيل من أن في اشتراط كلية الكبرى وإيجاب الصغرى بحثا، أما في الأول فلأنه إذا ثبت الأوسط للأصغر، وثبت الأكبر لأكثر أفراد الأوسط يحصل الطن بثبوت الأصغر للأكبر إلحاقاً للفرد بالأعم الأغلب كما في الاستقراء، وأما في الثاني فلأنهم صرحوا بأن الموجبة السالبة المحمول تصلح صغرى للشكل الأول، والسالبة تستلزمها فينبغي أن تصلح لذلك، غاية ما في الباب أن ظهور الإنتاج بملاحظة الإيجاب، وذلك لا يقضي أن يسلب عن السالبة صلاحيته لصغرى الأول فمدفوع، أما الأول فلأنه إذا ثبت الأكبر لأكثر الأوسط، فإما أن يحصل الظن بثبوته لكله فكلية الكبرى حاصلة، غاية ما في الباب أن تكون كليتها ظنية، وإن لم يحصل الظن بذلك لا يكون الاستدلال بحال الكلي على الجزئي فلا يكون قياساً، وأما في الثاني فلان الإنتاج حينئذ بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساواة، وهو أن لازم اللازم لازم ولولا صدقها لم يكن إنتاج الموجبة السالبة المحمول موجباً لإنتاج السالبة.
Its forms are five: The first is that one knows a positive or negative judgment for every individual of something which is the middle term, then one knows its affirmation for another [the minor term] wholly or partially, so one knows the affirmation of that judgment for the other likewise, certainly, obtained by intuition. Thus it refers to the universality of the major premise in the first figure, and the affirmation of its minor premise together with their actuality, and to its four necessary conclusions from its four modes by necessary implication.

Commentary: 'His saying: (Thus it refers to the universality of the major etc.) by his saying: for every individual of something, and the affirmation of its minor together with its actuality by his saying: then one knows its affirmation, where he specified affirmation by mention. Then the actuality of the minor is a condition according to the opinion of Ibn Sina, who considered the categorical proposition as actual; but if the categorical proposition is considered as possible, as is the opinion of al-Farabi, then the possible minor produces a conclusion in the first figure. And it has been said that there is a discussion regarding the condition of the universality of the major and the affirmation of the minor. As for the first, because if the middle term is affirmed of the minor, and the major is affirmed for most individuals of the middle, one obtains the opinion that the minor is affirmed for the major, by analogy to the individual to the common and predominant, as in induction. As for the second, because they explicitly stated that the affirmative with a negative predicate may serve as a minor in the first figure, and a negative premise implies it, so it should also be suitable for that; the utmost in the matter is that the appearance of the conclusion is by considering affirmation, and that does not require that the negative be deprived of its suitability as a minor of the first figure; thus it is refuted. As for the first: because if the major is affirmed for most of the middle, either probability of its affirmation for all of it obtains, so the universality of the major is obtained, the utmost being that its universality is probable; and if probability of that does not obtain, then the inference is not by applying the universal to the particular, so it is not qiyas. As for the second: because the conclusion then is by means of an extraneous premise, as in the qiyas of equality, which is that the necessary of the necessary is necessary; and if it were not true, the production of the affirmative with a negative predicate would not lead to the production of the negative.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage describes the first figure (shakl awwal) of qiyas, which is the first of five forms. The author states that in this figure, one knows a judgment (positive or negative) for every individual of the middle term, then knows its affirmation for the minor term (wholly or partially), thus knowing the judgment for the minor. This implies a universal major premise (kulliyya al-kubra) and an affirmative minor (ijab al-sughra) in actuality (fi'liyya). It also yields four necessary conclusions corresponding to the four modes. The commentary explains that the condition of actuality of the minor is according to Ibn Sina, who requires the categorical proposition (waḍ' al-waḍ') to be actual; al-Farabi allows the possible minor to produce a conclusion. Then an objection is raised: some question the necessity of a universal major and an affirmative minor. The author answers both objections. First objection: if the major is only affirmed for most individuals of the middle, one might get only probability, not certainty. The answer: if probability obtains for all, then the major is universal but probable; if not, the inference is not a proper qiyas. Second objection: an affirmative premise with a negative predicate (e.g., 'A is non-B') can serve as a minor. The answer: such a premise yields a conclusion only through an extraneous premise (like the qiyas of equality), so it does not undermine the requirement of an affirmative minor for direct qiyas. Thus the conditions of the first figure are defended.

صغرى
فعلي
ابن سينا
الفارابي
قياس المساواة
لازم
ظن
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a negative minor premise can produce a conclusion in the first figure directly, without any extra steps.
Correction: The text shows that a negative minor requires an extraneous premise (qiyas al-musawa) to yield a conclusion, so the direct condition of an affirmative minor remains necessary for straightforward first figure reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's view as presented in the commentary, what condition must the minor premise of the first figure fulfill?
A) It must be negative
B) It must be affirmative and actual
C) It must be possible
D) It must be universal

Key Takeaway
The first figure of qiyas requires a universal major premise and an affirmative actual minor; objections that challenge these conditions are resolved through distinctions in probability and the role of extraneous premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you construct an argument about a religious matter, do you ensure your premises are certain and apply to every relevant case? If you rely on 'most' or probability, is your conclusion truly reliable? How will you verify your reasoning from now on?
?
The passage shows that even great scholars like Ibn Sina and al-Farabi disagree on a fine condition. Are you quick to accept an argument just because it sounds logical? How often do you examine the hidden assumptions (like actuality) that could change the conclusion?
?
The objection about a negative minor being allowed was refuted using an extra premise. In your own life, when someone challenges your belief, do you honestly examine whether your reply introduces hidden assumptions that may not be justified? Commit to one area of your faith where you will test your arguments against the strict conditions of certainty.

The Second Figure of Qiyas and Its Conditions
Definition of the second figure with its two conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second figure of qiyas and its necessary conditions: a universal major premise and premises that differ in quality such that they cannot both be true or both false.

Core Idea
The second figure forces a negative conclusion through contradictory premises.

The second figure of qiyas turns our attention to a different pattern: instead of combining two affirmative premises, it requires one affirmative and one negative. This structural difference ensures a necessary logical consequence. Let's examine the precise conditions the author sets for this figure.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the second figure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author describes knowing a judgment about the major term and its opposite about the minor.
The key words are 'salb' (negation) and 'ījāb' (affirmation).
2
Identify the two required conditions
Look for the conditions: universality of the major premise (kulliyyat al-kubra) and difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative) to the extent that both cannot be true or both false.
The commentary explains what 'such that it is impossible' means.
3
Understand why these conditions are necessary
Consider why the premises must be contradictory in truth value and not just formally opposite. The conclusion in the second figure is always negative.
4
Test your understanding
Try to restate the condition in your own words or construct an example that satisfies it.
Example: 'Every human is mortal. No stone is mortal. Therefore, no stone is human?' (Actually, that's not second figure; second figure has the middle as predicate in both premises.) Better: 'Every human is mortal. No animal is mortal. Therefore, no animal is human.' But check the form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية أن يعلم حكم إيجابي أو سلبي لكل أفراد شيء هو الأكبر ومقابله لآخر كله أو بعضه فيعلم سلب ذلك الشيء عن الآخر كله أو بعضه فظهر أن الشكل الثاني يجب فيه كلية الكبرى، واختلاف مقدمتيه سلباً وإيجاباً بحيث يمتنع

قوله : (بحيث يمتنع إلخ) يعني لا يكفي في إنتاجه اختلاف مقدمتيه من حيث الصورة فقط، ولا بحيث يمتنع ارتفاعهما فقط
The second [form] is that one knows a positive or negative judgment for every individual of something which is the major term, and its opposite for another [the minor term] wholly or partially, so one knows the negation of that thing from the other wholly or partially. Thus it becomes clear that in the second figure, the universality of the major is required, and the difference of its two premises in negativity and positivity such that it is impossible [for both to be affirmative or both negative].

Commentary: 'His saying: (such that it is impossible etc.) means that it is not sufficient for its production that the two premises differ in form only, nor [only] such that their simultaneity is impossible.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage describes the second figure (shakl thani) of qiyas. The author explains that in this figure, one knows a judgment (positive or negative) for every individual of the major term, and the opposite of that judgment for the minor term (wholly or partially), thereby knowing the negation of the major term from the minor. This requires a universal major premise (kulliyya al-kubra) and that the two premises differ in quality: one affirmative and one negative, such that it is impossible for both to be affirmative or both negative (i.e., they are contradictory in quality). The commentary clarifies that the difference must be actual opposition in both form and truth value: it is not enough that the premises differ only in form (e.g., one affirmative, one negative) without actual opposition, nor is it enough that they are merely not both true (i.e., at least one false). Rather, they must be such that they cannot both be true or both false? Actually, 'يمتنع ارتفاعهما فقط' would mean that it is impossible for both to be false; but the commentary says it is not enough to have only that, nor only a formal difference. The exact meaning: the difference must be such that the premises are opposed in truth value (one true, one false) and also in quality, i.e., they are truly contradictory. The second figure aims to produce a negative conclusion (salb) by showing that the minor does not possess the major, through the contradictory premises.

Key Terms

كبرى
سلب
إيجاب
مقدمتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any difference in quality between the premises (one affirmative, one negative) is sufficient for a valid second-figure syllogism.
Correction: The text explicitly requires that the difference must be such that the premises cannot both be true or both false. A formal difference alone does not guarantee a necessary conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is required of the two premises in the second figure of qiyas?
A) Both must be affirmative
B) Both must be universal
C) They must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) such that they cannot both be true or both false
D) They must be singular propositions

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires a universal major premise and premises that are contradictory in quality and truth value, yielding a negative conclusion.

First Figure: Definition and Conditions
Defines the first figure and its three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the first figure of the syllogism and its three conditions: universal major, affirmative minor, and actuality of the premises.

Core Idea
The first figure is the basic valid syllogism requiring universal major, affirmative minor, and actual premises.

In this passage, the author defines the first figure of the syllogism—the foundational form of logical reasoning. He identifies its essential conditions and explains a key disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi regarding the actuality of the minor premise. Understanding these conditions is critical for evaluating syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms for major, minor, universality, and affirmation.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage states that the first figure requires a universal major premise, an affirmative minor premise, and actuality of the premises. Find these in the text.
The condition of actuality is stated as a point of difference between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi.
3
Understand the debate on actuality
Ibn Sina requires the minor premise to be actually affirmed, while al-Farabi allows it to be possible. Reflect on how this affects the validity of the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الرابع: القياس وهو العمدة الإيجابي أو السلبي ( للآخر كذلك ) أي لكله أو بعضه ( قطعاً) حاصلاً بالبديهة، فقد أشار إلى كلية كبرى الشكل الأول، وإيجاب صغراه مع فعليتهما وإلى نتائجه الأربع اللازمة من ضروبه بالأربعة لزوماً ضرورياً قوله : فقد أشار إلى كلية كبرى إلخ) بقوله : لكل أفراد شيء وإيجاب صغراه مع فعليتها بقوله ثم يعلم ثبوته حيث خص الثبوت بالذكر، ثم فعلية الصغرى شرط على رأي ابن سينا، حيث اعتبر عقد الوضع بالفعل، وأما إذا اعتبر عقد الوضع بالإمكان كما هو رأي الفارابي فالصغرى الممكنة منتج في الشكل الأول
The sixth observation – the fourth purpose: analogy, which is the mainstay. The affirmative or negative (for the other likewise), i.e., for all of it or part of it, (absolutely) obtained by intuition; thus he alluded to the universality of the major of the first figure, and the affirmation of its minor with their actuality, and to its four necessary conclusions from its four modes by necessary necessity. His statement: 'he alluded to the universality of the major etc.' by his statement: 'for each individual of a thing,' and the affirmation of its minor with its actuality by his statement: 'then he knows its existence where he specified existence by mention,' then the actuality of the minor is a condition according to the opinion of Ibn Sina, for he considered the actual positing of the minor, but if the positing of the minor is considered by possibility as is the opinion of al-Farabi, then the possible minor is productive in the first figure.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of the syllogism and outlines its conditions. The author states that the first figure is when one knows a universal judgment (affirmative or negative) about each individual of a thing, and then knows that same judgment applies to another thing entirely or partially, resulting in a conclusion that is necessary and evident. The commentary explains that the author's phrase 'alluded to' refers to the universality of the major premise and the affirmation of the minor premise with actuality. It notes that the actuality of the minor is a condition according to Ibn Sina, but al-Farabi allows possibility, meaning the minor can be possible and still produce a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present an argument for a core belief of yours, do you ensure that your premises are as certain as possible? How does the condition of 'actuality' challenge you to ground your reasoning in reality, not just possibility?
?
Are there areas in your life where you accept conclusions without verifying that the premises are truly universal and affirmative? What would change if you held your beliefs to the standard of the first figure?
?
The first figure produces necessary conclusions. Is there a truth you are avoiding because proving it would require premises you are unwilling to examine? What would it cost you to examine them?

Objection on First Figure Conditions and Refutation
Refutes the objection that universality of major and affirmation of minor are not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of two objections concerning the conditions of universality of the major and affirmation of the minor in the first figure of the syllogism.

Core Idea
First-figure syllogisms require a universal major and an affirmative minor to deliver certainty.

In this passage, the author addresses two common objections about the conditions of the first figure. The first objection suggests that probable premises can still produce a syllogism, while the second questions why a negative proposition cannot serve as the minor. The refutation clarifies the necessity of these conditions for certainty and distinguishes between probable inference and genuine syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: objection first, then refutation.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two points of objection: one about universality of the major, the other about affirmation of the minor. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The objection regarding the major uses induction as an analogy.
3
Understand the refutations
See how the author refutes each objection: the first by showing that probability is not certainty, the second by introducing the need for a foreign premise.
The refutation of the second introduces the syllogism of equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل من أن في اشتراط كلية الكبرى وإيجاب الصغرى بحثا، أما في الأول فلأنه إذا ثبت الأوسط للأصغر، وثبت الأكبر لأكثر أفراد الأوسط يحصل الطن بثبوت الأصغر للأكبر إلحاقاً للفرد بالأعم الأغلب كما في الاستقراء، وأما في الثاني فلأنهم صرحوا بأن الموجبة السالبة المحمول تصلح صغرى للشكل الأول، والسالبة تستلزمها فينبغي أن تصلح لذلك، غاية ما في الباب أن ظهور الإنتاج بملاحظة الإيجاب، وذلك لا يقضي أن يسلب عن السالبة صلاحيته لصغرى الأول فمدفوع، أما الأول فلأنه إذا ثبت الأكبر لأكثر الأوسط، فإما أن يحصل الظن بثبوته لكله فكلية الكبرى ،حاصلة، غاية ما في الباب أن تكون كليتها ظنية، وإن لم يحصل الظن بذلك لا يكون الاستدلال بحال الكلي على الجزئي فلا يكون قياساً، وأما في الثاني فلان الإنتاج حينئذ بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساواة، وهو أن لازم اللازم لازم ولولا صدقها لم يكن إنتاج الموجبة السالبة المحمول موجباً لإنتاج السالبة.
And what is said that there is a discussion concerning the condition of the universality of the major and the affirmation of the minor: as for the first, because if the middle is established for the minor, and the major is established for most individuals of the middle, then opinion is obtained of the establishment of the minor for the major, annexing the individual to the more general and prevalent, as in induction. And as for the second, because they explicitly stated that the affirmative with a negated predicate is suitable as a minor for the first figure, and the negative implies it, so it should be suitable for that. The most that can be said is that the appearance of productivity is by considering affirmation, and that does not entail depriving the negative of its suitability as a minor of the first. So it is refuted. As for the first, because if the major is established for most of the middle, then either opinion is obtained of its establishment for all of it, so the universality of the major is obtained—the most that can be said is that its universality is probable—and if opinion is not obtained for that, then the inference is not by the state of the universal over the particular, so it is not a syllogism. And as for the second, because productivity then is by means of a foreign premise, as in the syllogism of equality, which is that the consequent of a consequent is a consequent, and without its truth, the productivity of the affirmative with a negated predicate would not cause the productivity of the negative.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding the conditions of universality of the major and affirmation of the minor in the first figure. The objection raises two points: first, if the major is known for most individuals of the middle term, why can't we get a probable conclusion? Second, why can't a negative proposition serve as the minor, since a negative proposition is equivalent to an affirmative with a negated predicate? The refutation answers: first, if the major holds for most of the middle, we may get a probable universal major, but the conclusion would be probable, not certain; if we don't get probability, it's not a syllogism. Second, the inference from a negative minor actually requires an additional premise (like the principle of transitivity) and is not a direct first-figure syllogism.

Key Terms
كلية
إيجاب
استقراء
ظن
مقدمة أجنبية
قياس المساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first figure can produce certainty with only probable premises or with a negative minor.
Correction: The refutation shows that probability yields only probable conclusions, and a negative minor requires an additional premise, so they are not valid first-figure syllogisms yielding certainty.

Checkpoint

C) It says the first figure only works with affirmative premises

Key Takeaway
The first figure requires a universal major and an affirmative minor to achieve certainty; deviations result in probable inference or require extra premises.

Second Figure: Definition and Four Modes
Defines the second figure and enumerates its four modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the second figure of the syllogism, its two necessary conditions (universal major and difference in quality), and how its four modes produce only negative conclusions.

Core Idea
The second figure requires a universal major and opposite premises; it only yields negative conclusions.

Having covered the first figure and its four modes, we now turn to the second figure. This passage from al-Ījī defines the second figure, explains its essential conditions—a universal major and premises differing in quality—and then enumerates its four valid modes. All modes of this figure produce only negative conclusions, a key result that requires careful reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms: كبرى (major), صغرى (minor), سلب (negation), إيجاب (affirmation).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two conditions
The text states two conditions for the second figure: (1) the major must be universal (كلية الكبرى), and (2) the two premises must differ in quality (اختلاف المقدمتين سلباً وإيجاباً). Understand why these are necessary.
If the major were particular, the syllogism would be invalid.
3
List the four modes
Based on the quality of the major, the minor takes the opposite quality. If the major is affirmative, the minor is negative (universal or particular). If the major is negative, the minor is affirmative (universal or particular). Count the four combinations.
This mirrors the four modes of the first figure.
4
Understand the conclusion type
The text says the second figure produces only negative conclusions (سلب كلياً أو جزئياً). Reflect on why: if the thing (major term) were affirmed of the minor, opposite judgments would combine, which is impossible.
This is the key insight of the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثانية أن يعلم حكم) إيجابي أو سلبي لكل أفراد شيء ) هو الأكبر ( ومقابله ) أي ويعلم مقابل ذلك الحكم ( لآخر) وهو الأصغر ) كله أو بعضه فيعلم سلب ذلك الشيء عن الآخر) كله أو بعضه فظهر أن الشكل الثاني يجب فيه كلية الكبرى، واختلاف مقدمتيه سلباً وإيجاباً بحيث يمتنع اجتماعهما في شيء واحد، فتكون ضروبه أيضاً أربعة وأنه لا ينتج إلا سلبا كلياً أو جزئيا يحتاج في العلم بلزومه إلى نوع تأمل، وهو أن يكون ذلك الشيء لو كان ثابتا للآخر لاجتمع فيه الحكمان المتقابلان قوله : ( فتكون ضروبه أيضاً أربعة لأن الكبرى الكلية إذا كانت موجبة، فالصغرى إما سالبة كلية أو جزئية، وإذا كانت سالبة فالصغرى إما موجبة كلية أو جزئية .
(The second is that one knows a judgment) affirmative or negative for all individuals of a thing, which is the major, (and its opposite), i.e., and one knows the opposite of that judgment for another, which is the minor, all of it or part of it, so one knows the negation of that thing from the other, all of it or part of it. Thus it appears that the second figure requires universality of the major, and the difference of its two premises in negation and affirmation such that their conjunction in one thing is impossible. So its modes are also four, and it produces only a universal or particular negation, requiring in knowing its necessity a kind of reflection, which is that if that thing were established for the other, the two opposite judgments would combine in it. His statement: (So its modes are also four) because if the universal major is affirmative, then the minor is either universal negative or particular negative, and if it is negative, then the minor is either universal affirmative or particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the syllogism. One knows a positive or negative judgment about all individuals of a thing (the major), and knows the opposite judgment about another thing (the minor) entirely or partially, so that the thing is negated from the other. Thus, the second figure requires a universal major and that the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) such that they cannot both be true of the same thing. It has four modes, producing only negative conclusions (universal or particular). The conclusion's necessity requires reflection: if the thing were true of the other, the two opposite judgments would coexist. The commentary explains the four modes: if the universal major is affirmative, the minor can be universal negative or particular negative; if the major is negative, the minor can be universal affirmative or particular affirmative.

Key Terms

كلية الكبرى
اختلاف المقدمتين
سلب
إيجاب
ضروب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second figure can produce affirmative conclusions.
Correction: The text explicitly states that the second figure 'produces only a universal or particular negation' (لا ينتج إلا سلبا كلياً أو جزئياً). Affirmative conclusions are impossible because they would require both premises to be true of the same subject, which is prevented by their difference in quality.

Key Takeaway

Third Figure: Definition and Six Modes
Defines the third figure and enumerates its six valid modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the third figure of the syllogism and the six modes that yield particular conclusions.

Core Idea
The third figure only yields particular conclusions, never universal.

This passage explains the third figure of the categorical syllogism. You will learn how the middle term functions and what combinations of premises produce valid conclusions. The text emphasizes that the third figure always yields particular conclusions, whether affirmative or negative, and specifies the six valid modes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the general structure of the third figure. Note how the middle term relates to the minor and major terms.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The text states that at least one premise must be universal. Also, the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (not potential).
This is a key rule for the third figure.
3
Understand the three affirmative modes
The commentary lists the three affirmative modes: (1) both premises universal affirmative, (2) minor universal affirmative with major particular affirmative, (3) minor particular affirmative with major universal affirmative.
All yield particular affirmative conclusions.
4
Understand the three negative modes
The commentary lists the three negative modes: (1) minor universal affirmative with major universal negative, (2) minor universal affirmative with major particular negative, (3) minor particular affirmative with major universal negative.
All yield particular negative conclusions.
5
Recognize the overall limitation
Conclude that the third figure can never produce a universal conclusion. This is a distinctive feature compared to the first two figures.
This is a common point of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالثة أن يعلم ثبوت أمرين هما الأصغر والأكبر (الثالث ) هو الأوسط ولا بد أن يكون ثبوتهما أو ثبوت أحدهما لذلك الثالث كلياً، ( فيعلم) حينئذ ( التقاؤهما فيه ) أي في ذلك الثالث إما كله أو بعضه ) ولا يعلم ) التقاؤهما (فيما عداه ) بل يجوز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر، فلا يصدق عليه كلياً ( لا جرم كان اللازم (جزئياً موجباً في ضروب ثلاثة، وأما الضابط فيما ينتج منه السلب فهو أن يعلم ثبوت أحد أمرين لشيء إما كلياً أو جزئياً، ويعلم مع الأول سلب الآخر عن ذلك الشيء أو بعضه، ويعلم مع الثاني سلب الآخر عن الشيء كلياً، فيعلم سلب الآخر عن صاحبه في ذلك الشيء، ولا يعلم فيما عداه فيحصل ضروب ثلاثة أخرى منتجة للسلب الجزئي، ويظهر من ذلك كله أن الشكل الثالث لا بد فيه من كلية إحدى المقدمتين، وإيجاب الصغرى مع فعليتها وأنه لا قوله : ( ضروب ثلاثة) هي الموجبتان الكليتان والصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية أو بالعكس. قوله : ( ضرورب ثلاثة أخرى هي الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، أو الجزئية والصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية .
(The third is that one knows the existence of two things, which are the minor and the major, (for a third), which is the middle, and it is necessary that their existence or the existence of one of them for that third is universal; (then one knows) at that time (their meeting in it), i.e., in that third, either all of it or part of it, (and one does not know) their meeting (beyond it), rather it is permissible that the minor is more general than the major, so it is not true of it universally. (Hence the conclusion is) particular affirmative in three modes. And as for the rule for what produces negation from it, it is that one knows the establishment of one of two things for a thing either universally or particularly, and knows with the first the negation of the other from that thing or part of it, and knows with the second the negation of the other from the thing universally, so one knows the negation of the other from its companion in that thing, and does not know beyond it. So three other modes result producing particular negation. And it appears from all that that the third figure must have universality of one of the two premises, and affirmation of the minor with its actuality, and that it does not... His statement: '(three modes) are the two universal affirmatives, and the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major, or vice versa.' His statement: '(three other modes) are the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major, or the particular negative, and the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major.'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the syllogism. One knows that two things (the minor and major) both belong to a third thing (the middle), either universally or partially. Then one knows their conjunction in that third, but not beyond. The conclusion is particular affirmative in three modes. For negative conclusions, the rule is: if one establishes something for a thing, and with the first, negates the other from that thing, or with the second, negates the other completely, then one knows the negation of the other from its companion in that thing. This yields three other modes producing particular negative conclusions. Thus the third figure requires at least one universal premise, an affirmative minor, and actuality. The commentary specifies the exact combinations of premises for the three affirmative and three negative modes.

جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third figure can produce universal conclusions.
Correction: The text explicitly states that the conclusion is always particular (جزئياً) in both affirmative and negative modes.

Checkpoint

A) Both premises must be universal
B) At least one premise must be universal
C) Both premises must be particular
D) The minor must be universal

Key Takeaway
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion, requiring at least one universal premise and an affirmative minor.

Types of Affirmation: Real and Judgmental
Sanusi explains that affirmation can be real or judgmental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and judgmental affirmation in the minor premise of the first figure, and why some negative propositions can function as affirmatives.

Core Idea
Affirmation can be real or judgmental; even negatives can be equivalent to affirmatives.

The author clarifies that the affirmation required in the minor premise of the first figure is not limited to standard affirmative propositions. It includes both real affirmatives and judgmental affirmatives, such as negative propositions that are equivalent to affirmatives with a negated predicate. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's technical language.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the author distinguishes 'real' and 'judgmental' affirmation. Understand the difference: real is a standard affirmative; judgmental includes negatives that are equivalent to affirmatives.
The example uses 'No J is B' and 'Everything not B is A'.
3
Understand the equivalence
The author explains that a standard negative and a negative-predicate proposition are equal in not requiring the subject's existence. This allows them to be treated as affirmations.
This is key to why judgmental affirmation works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطرق الثلاثة كيف كانت مقدماتها ولا يصح سلب صلاحية الصغروية والكبروية من المقدمات التي قالوا بصلاحيتها لهما، وقد يقال : الإيجاب الذي يشترط في صغرى الشكل الأول أعم من أن يكون حقيقة كما في الموجبة المحصلة، والمعدولة السالبة المحمول أو حكماً كالسالبة المحصلة التي في قوة موجبة سالبة المحمول فإن جميعها ينتج بشرط أن يوافقه موضوع الكبرى، كقولنا لا شيء من ج ب وكل ما هو ليس ب ا فإنه يوافق كل ج هو ليس ب والصغرى في حكمه لأن السالبة والسالبة المحمول متساويان في عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع، وحكم أحد المتساويين حكم الآخر، وهذا قول الخونجي والأرموي أولاً، ثم رجع الأرموي وبنى رجوعه على ما بنى وأجاب الجد عن مبناه في فصول ،البدائع، فمن أراد التفصيل فلينظر ثمة . قوله : ( وأما الضابط فيما ينتج منه السلب فإن قلت : لم لم يتعرض المصنف للضروب المنتجة للسلب قلت : لأن أقرب الأشكال إلى الطبع هو الشكل الأول وأقربها إليه بعد الأول هو الثاني، وكذا ذكر ضروبهما بتمامها وأبعدها عنه هو الرابع، ولذا لم يذكره أصلاً وأما الشكل الثالث فلما كان أقرب إليه بالنسبة إلى الرابع وأبعد بالنسبة إلى الثاني تعرض لأشرف ضروبه وهو المنتج للإيجاب، ولم يتعرض لأخسها .
The three types, however their premises are, and it is not valid to negate the suitability of being minor and major from the premises that they said are suitable for them. And it may be said: The affirmation that is required in the minor of the first figure is more general than being real, as in the standard affirmative and the modified negative predicate, or judgmental, as in the standard negative which is equivalent to an affirmative with a negated predicate. For all of them produce provided that the subject of the major agrees with it, as when we say 'Nothing from J is B' and 'Everything that is not B is A', for it agrees that 'every J is not B' and the minor is in its ruling because the standard negative and the negative predicate are equal in not requiring the existence of the subject, and the ruling of one of two equals is the ruling of the other. This is the statement of al-Khunaji and al-Urmawi first, then al-Urmawi retracted and built his retraction on what he built, and al-Jadd answered his foundation in the chapters of al-Bada'i'. So whoever wants detail, let him look there. His statement: (And as for the rule regarding what produces negation from it:) If you said: Why did the author not mention the modes that produce negation? I said: Because the closest of the figures to nature is the first figure, and the closest to it after the first is the second, and likewise he mentioned their modes completely, and the farthest from it is the fourth, so he did not mention it at all. As for the third figure, since it is closer to it relative to the fourth and farther relative to the second, he mentioned its nobler modes, which are those producing affirmation, and did not mention its baser ones.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage discusses two points. First, it clarifies that the affirmation required in the minor premise of the first figure is broader: it can be real (as in a standard affirmative proposition) or judgmental (as in a negative proposition that is equivalent to an affirmative with a negated predicate). It gives the example of 'No J is B' and 'Everything that is not B is A' where the minor is equivalent. It mentions the views of al-Khūnajī and al-Urmawī and al-Jadd. Second, it answers why the author did not mention the negative modes of the third figure: because the first figure is the most natural, then the second, the third is farther; the author mentioned the modes of the first and second fully, but for the third he only mentioned the nobler positive modes and omitted the baser negative ones.

Key Terms
إيجاب حقيقي
إيجاب حكمي
سالبة محصلة
موجبة سالبة المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only standard affirmative propositions can be minors in the first figure.
Correction: Judgmental affirmatives, including certain negatives, can also serve as minors.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'judgmental affirmation'?
A) An affirmative proposition that is actually negative
B) A negative proposition that is equivalent to an affirmative with a negated predicate
C) An affirmative proposition that is only true by convention
D) A proposition that is both affirmative and negative

Key Takeaway
Affirmation in logic includes both real affirmatives and judgmental ones, allowing some negative propositions to function as affirmatives.

Reason for Omitting Negative Modes of the Third Figure
The author explains why negative modes of the third figure were omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author did not mention the negative modes of the third figure and how the four logical figures rank in closeness to human nature.

Core Idea
The author omits negative modes because they are baser and the third figure is less natural.

The text raises a question: why did the author not mention the modes that produce negation? The answer relates to the natural hierarchy of the logical figures: the first figure is most natural, then the second, then the third, and the fourth is farthest. The author only mentioned the nobler positive modes of the third figure, leaving out the baser negative ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the question: why were the negative modes not mentioned? And the answer: based on closeness to nature.
2
Understand the ranking of figures
The author ranks the figures: first (most natural), second, third, fourth (most remote). The third is closer than fourth but farther than second.
This ranking determines which modes are mentioned.
3
Apply the ranking to the omission
Since the third figure is less natural than first and second, only its nobler modes (producing affirmation) are mentioned, while the baser negative modes are omitted.
The author fully covers first and second figures, omits fourth entirely.

The Rule for Negative Moods in the Third Figure
Al-Iji explains the rule for negative moods in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rule that governs how the third figure produces particular negative conclusions.

Core Idea
The third figure uses a two-case rule to yield particular negative moods.

Al-Iji now turns to the negative moods of the third figure. He provides a precise rule based on the scope of affirmation and negation. This lesson unpacks that rule step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the rule.
Notice that the rule involves two main cases.
2
Identify the two cases
The rule first considers an affirmation (universal or particular) and then distinguishes whether the negation is attached to the first or second case.
3
See the resulting moods
From these cases, three moods that produce particular negation are obtained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضابط فيما ينتج منه السلب فهو أن يعلم ثبوت أحد أمرين لشيء إما كلياً أو جزئياً، ويعلم مع الأول سلب الآخر عن ذلك الشيء أو بعضه، ويعلم مع الثاني سلب الآخر عن الشيء كلياً، فيعلم سلب الآخر عن صاحبه في ذلك الشيء، ولا يعلم فيما عداه فيحصل ضروب ثلاثة أخرى منتجة للسلب الجزئي، ويظهر من ذلك كله أن الشكل الثالث لا بد فيه من كلية إحدى المقدمتين، وإيجاب الصغرى مع فعليتها وأنه لا ينتج إلا جزئياً موجباً أو سلباً، وإنما لم يتعرض للشكل الرابع لأنه بعيد عن الطبع يحتاج في بيان استلزامه للنتيجة إلى مؤنة ربما كانت أكثر مما يحتاج إليه في تحصيل تلك النتيجة ابتداء من غيره
As for the rule for what produces negative [conclusions] from it: it is that one knows the affirmation of one of two matters to something, either universal or particular, and with the first he knows the negation of the other from that thing or some of it, and with the second he knows the negation of the other from the thing universally; thus he knows the negation of the other from its companion in that thing, and he does not know [the negation] in what other than it. Thus three other moods are obtained that produce particular negation. And it appears from all this that the third figure necessarily requires universality of one of the two premises, and affirmation of the minor with its actuality, and that it produces only a particular affirmative or negative. And he did not discuss the fourth figure because it is remote from the natural disposition, needing in clarifying its entailment of the conclusion an effort perhaps greater than what is needed to obtain that conclusion directly from other [figures].
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the rule for producing negative conclusions in the third figure of the syllogism. The author first gives the method: to know that one of two things is affirmed of something (universally or particularly) and that the other is negated from it (completely or partially) under specific conditions, resulting in three moods that produce particular negative conclusions. He then summarises that the third figure requires universality of one premise, affirmation of the minor, and actuality, yielding only particular (not universal) conclusions. Finally, he explains why the fourth figure is not discussed: it is unnatural and requires more effort to derive the conclusion than to obtain it directly from other figures.

Key Terms
ضابط
سلب
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the third figure can produce universal negative conclusions.
Correction: Al-Iji clarifies that the third figure only yields particular negation, not universal.

Checkpoint
According to the rule, what results from the two cases?
A) Three moods producing universal negation
B) Three moods producing particular negation
C) Two moods producing particular negation
D) Four moods producing universal negation

Key Takeaway
The third figure's negative moods are governed by a rule that yields only particular negative conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Constraints of the Third Figure and Omission of the Fourth
Al-Iji summarises the third figure's constraints and justifies omitting the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary conditions of the third figure and why al-Iji did not discuss the fourth figure.

Core Idea
The third figure requires universal premises and yields only particulars; the fourth figure is omitted as unnatural.

Having explained the moods, al-Iji now summarises the essential features of the third figure: it must have a universal premise, an affirmative minor, and only produces particular conclusions. He then explains why he omits the fourth figure—it is contrived and requires more effort than direct reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to identify the constraints and the reason for omitting the fourth figure.
2
List the constraints of the third figure
The passage mentions three requirements: universality of one premise, affirmation of the minor, and actuality of the minor. Also, it only produces particular conclusions.
3
Understand why the fourth figure is omitted
Al-Iji says the fourth figure is unnatural (بعيد عن الطبع) and requires more effort to prove its conclusion than to derive it from other figures.
This is a practical reason, not a logical invalidity.

The Third Method: الدوران (Co-Rotation)
Explains the third method of co-rotation and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of co-rotation for proving causality and its refutation that causality can be known without it.

Core Idea
Co-rotation is not always necessary to prove causality; causality can be known directly.

This lesson introduces the third method of proving causality, known as الدوران (co-rotation). The method claims that if two things co-rotate in existence and non-existence, they are cause and effect. The refutation presented here shows that in the case of motility and motion, the cause is known directly without relying on co-rotation, thus the method is not universally valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on co-rotation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents the method and then immediately raises an objection.
Pay attention to the example of motility and motion.
2
Understand the argument of co-rotation
Identify the core claim: if A co-rotates with B, then A is the cause of B. The text illustrates this with the example of motion and motility.
The method is used to prove causality, but it opens the door to doubt if not properly constrained.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation gives two responses: (1) if motility and motion are not distinct, no co-rotation exists; (2) if they are distinct, then motion is defined as that which necessitates motility, so causality is known without co-rotation.
Thus, the method is not needed in this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية مسألة العالمية في تزييف دليل الفلاسفة على كونه تعالى عالماً بالكليات، (الثالث الدوران لو لم يفد كون المدار علة للدائر، وجاز معه أن يكون الدائر معللاً بغير المدار ( لجاز استناد المتحركية إلى ( علة ( غير الحركة) مع دوران الأولى على الثانية وجوداً ،وعدماً، وذلك فتح لباب التشكيك في العلل والمعلولات (قلنا: إن سلم التغاير بين المتحركية والحركة أي لا تغاير بينهما عندنا، فلا يتصور هناك دوران وعلية، ولئن سلمنا كما هو مذهب مثبتي الأحوال ( فلا نريد بالحركة إلا ما يوجب المتحركية ) فإذا قيل : لنا جوزوا إسناد المتحركية، إلى غير الحركة كان معناه جوزوا أن يكون الموجب للمتحركية غير ما هو موجب لها وفساده ظاهر، والحاصل أن العلية ها هنا معلومة مع قطع النظر عن الدوران، فلا يلزم من القدح في دلالته على العلية القدح في العلية المعلومة بوجه آخر (الرابع) المقارن) الذي زعمتم أنه يجوز أن العلة للدائر ( إن لازم المدار وساواه بحيث لا ينفك أحدهما الآخر يكون هو [؟] عن [؟] كان [؟] ( ) حصل المطلوب ) الذي هو الحكم إذ كلما وجد المدار، وجد المقارن وكلما وجد المقارن وجد الحكم المطلوب الذي هو قبح الفعل الدائر مع تلك الوجوه مثلاً ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يلازمه ولم يساوه لم يكن هذا الذي فرضناه مداراً ( مداراً ) لأنه إن المقارن أخص لم يكن المدار مداراً وجوداً، وإن كان أعم لم يكن المدار مداراً عدماً هذا خلف ( قلنا : لعل المدار (لازم) للمقارن ( أعم ) منه ( فيوجد ) المدار ( دونه في صورة النزاع ) أي نختار أن المقارن أخص من المدار موجود معه فيما عدا المتنازع فيه، فيوجد الحكم هناك وغير موجود معه في صورة النزاع فلا يوجد الحكم هاهنا مع كونه مداراً له وجوداً وعدماً فيما عداها من الصور، ودعوى كونه مداراً له في هذه الصورة أيضاً مصادرة على المطلوب ( وثانيها ) أي ثاني الأمور التي هي أشهر الطرق ، قوله : ( المقارن) إلخ) حاصله أن المقصود من إثبات علية المدار بالدوران تعدية الحكم وهو حاصل على تقدير كون المقارن علة فهذا المنع لا يضرنا هذا تقدير أن يشترط في العلة كونها مؤثرة، وأما على تقدير الاكتفاء بكونه موجباً ومستلزماً للحكم على ما نقلناه سابقاً فالتقرير ظاهر. قوله: (مصادرة على المطلوب لأن ثبوت الحكم في الصورة المخصوصة بالدوران موقوف على ثبوت الدوران، وثبوت الدوران حينئذ يكون موقوفا على دعوى الدوران وثبوته في هذه الصورة. وهذا الوجه لا يدل عليه كما لا يخفى ويمكن التقصي بالتكلف فليتأمل .
The sixth observatory - the fifth aim: What are the strong methods? The universal issue in refuting the philosophers' proof that He, exalted, is knowing of universals. (The third: co-rotation. If it were not that the co-rotating thing is a cause for the rotating thing, and it were permissible that the rotating thing be caused by other than the co-rotating thing, then it would be permissible to attribute motility to a cause other than motion, while the first co-rotates with the second in existence and non-existence, and that is opening the door to doubt regarding causes and effects. We said: If the differentiation between motility and motion is granted—that is, there is no differentiation between them in our view—then no co-rotation and causality can be conceived there. And if we grant it, as is the doctrine of the affirmers of states, then we only mean by motion that which necessitates motility. So if it is said: Allow us to attribute motility to other than motion, it would mean allowing that the necessitator of motility is other than what necessitates it, and its absurdity is apparent. The conclusion is that causality here is known regardless of co-rotation, so impugning its indication of causality does not entail impugning the causality known by another means. (The fourth: the concomitant) which you claim is that the cause of the rotating thing is... if it is necessary for the co-rotating thing and equal to it such that one is not separated from the other, then the desired is obtained, which is the ruling—that whenever the co-rotating thing exists, the concomitant exists, and whenever the concomitant exists, the desired ruling exists, which is, for example, the ugliness of the rotating act with those aspects. (Otherwise) if it is not necessary and equal, then this thing we assumed to be a co-rotating thing is not a co-rotating thing because if the concomitant is more specific, the co-rotating thing is not a co-rotating thing in existence, and if it is more general, it is not a co-rotating thing in non-existence—this is a contradiction. We said: Perhaps the co-rotating thing is necessary for the concomitant, more general than it, so the co-rotating thing exists without it in the disputed case. That is, we choose that the concomitant is more specific than the co-rotating thing, existing with it in other than the disputed case, so the ruling exists there and does not exist with it in the disputed case, so the ruling does not exist here despite its being a co-rotating thing for it in existence and non-existence in cases other than that. The claim that it is a co-rotating thing for it in this case as well is a begging of the question. (And the second of them) that is, the second of the matters that are the most famous methods. His saying: (The concomitant, etc.) The gist is that the purpose of proving the causality of the co-rotating thing by co-rotation is to transfer the ruling, and that is obtained on the assumption that the concomitant is a cause, so this prevention does not harm us. This is on the assumption that a condition for the cause is that it be effective. But on the assumption of being content with it being a necessitator and entailer of the ruling, as we transmitted previously, the exposition is apparent. His saying: (Begging of the question because the establishment of the ruling in the specific case by co-rotation depends on the establishment of co-rotation, and the establishment of co-rotation then depends on the claim of co-rotation and its establishment in this case. And this aspect does not indicate it, as is not hidden, and one may avoid it with difficulty, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth methods of proving causality, namely الدوران (co-rotation) and the objection based on المقارن (concomitant). It begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' proof that God knows universals. The third method (الدوران) argues that if a thing (المدار) is not a cause for another (الدائر), then one could attribute motility to a cause other than motion, which would open doubt. The commentator answers: if no distinction exists between motility and motion, co-rotation is inconceivable; if one grants their distinction (as in the theory of أحوال), then motion is defined as what necessitates motility, so the cause is known without co-rotation. The fourth method (المقارن) is then introduced: the opponent claims that if the concomitant is co-extensive and co-occurrent with the co-rotating thing, then the ruling is established. If not, the co-rotating thing is not truly a co-rotating thing. The commentator replies that the co-rotating thing may be more general than the concomitant, so the ruling exists in other cases but not in the disputed case; claiming it does is begging the question. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the purpose of proving causality by co-rotation is to transfer the ruling, and the objection does not harm if one assumes the cause is effective or merely necessitates.

Key Terms
دوران
مدار
دائر
علة
معلول
تغاير
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Co-rotation alone is always sufficient to prove causality.
Correction: The passage shows that in some cases, causality is known directly and co-rotation is not needed; the method can be refuted.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation against the co-rotation method?
A) Co-rotation is always valid and cannot be questioned.
B) Causality can be known without co-rotation in the example given.
C) The example of motility and motion shows co-rotation is false.
D) The author rejects the theory of states.

Key Takeaway
Co-rotation is not always necessary to establish causality; causality can be known through other means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a proof you once relied on for a core belief is shown to be weak, do you immediately doubt the belief itself, or do you seek a stronger foundation? How does this lesson's distinction between the method and the known causality challenge your intellectual habits?
?
In your personal life, are there patterns you assume are causally linked (e.g., certain actions always lead to certain outcomes)? Could it be that you are relying on mere co-rotation rather than direct knowledge? What would it take for you to know such causality directly?
?
This passage warns that dismissing co-rotation without a stronger basis could 'open the door to doubt regarding causes and effects.' Where in your faith or worldview have you allowed a single weak argument to cast doubt on what you otherwise know to be true? Will you commit to re-examining that now?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
7 min
The Fourth Method: المقارن (The Concomitant)
Presents the objection of the concomitant and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection based on the concomitant (مقارن) against co-rotation and the counter-response.

Core Idea
The concomitant objection shows co-rotation fails unless the concomitant is co-extensive, and even then, the conclusion may be begged.

This lesson presents the fourth method, المقارن (the concomitant), which is an objection to the co-rotation argument. It argues that for co-rotation to prove causality, the concomitant must be necessary and equal to the co-rotating thing; otherwise, the method is invalid. The passage then provides a response: the co-rotating thing may be more general than the concomitant, and claiming it holds in the disputed case is begging the question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the concomitant
Read the Arabic text and translation. Focus on the condition that the concomitant must be necessary and equal to the co-rotating thing.
Look for the phrase 'إن لازم المدار وساواه'.
2
Understand the condition for co-rotation
The objector claims that if the concomitant is not co-extensive (more specific or more general), then the co-rotating thing is not truly co-rotating.
If the concomitant is more specific, the co-rotating thing exists without it; if more general, the co-rotating thing is absent in some cases.
3
Grasp the response and the charge of begging the question
The response says the co-rotating thing may be more general, so it exists without the concomitant in the disputed case, and claiming it is co-rotating there is a petitio principii.
The purpose of proving causality by co-rotation is to transfer the ruling, and this is salvaged if the concomitant is the cause.

Source Text

This passage presents the third and fourth methods of proving causality, namely الدوران (co-rotation) and the objection based on المقارن (concomitant). Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concomitant is always the cause of the co-rotating thing.
Correction: The passage shows that the concomitant may be more specific, and the co-rotating thing can exist without it, so it is not necessarily the cause.

Checkpoint
What condition does the objector require for the concomitant to validate co-rotation?
A) The concomitant must be more general than the co-rotating thing.
B) The concomitant must be necessary and equal to the co-rotating thing.
C) The concomitant must be more specific than the co-rotating thing.
D) The concomitant must be the effect of the co-rotating thing.

Key Takeaway
The validity of co-rotation depends on the co-extensiveness of the concomitant; otherwise, the argument begs the question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you were convinced that one thing caused another because they always seemed to occur together. Did you ever stop to consider whether the connection was truly co-extensive or just a coincidence? What would it mean for your worldview if you found you had been mistaken?
?
In your pursuit of knowledge about Allah, are you willing to examine the logical foundations of your proofs, even if it means challenging comfortable assumptions? How does this lesson's warning about circular reasoning confront your intellectual honesty?

Logic and Technical Terms

Definition of al-Sabr and Its Example
Defines al-sabr and provides an example of its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-sabr as a non-exhaustive division and see a concrete example of how it is applied to establish a common cause.

Core Idea
Al-sabr is a non-exhaustive division — it lists possibilities but may miss the real cause.

This lesson introduces the method of al-sabr (exploration), one of the strong methods used in kalam to establish the common cause (al-illa al-mushtaraka). We will examine its definition and a detailed example involving the visibility of the color black.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the definition and example are presented as a continuous argument.
The example lists five possible causes and eliminates all but one.
2
Identify the core definition
Locate how Sanusi defines al-sabr: 'a non-exhaustive division' (قسمة غير منحصرة). Understand what 'non-exhaustive' means here.
This means the list of possibilities may not include every conceivable cause.
3
Analyze the example structure
Break down the example: blackness being visible. The possible causes listed are: existence, being an accident, being originated, being a color, being blackness. All are rejected except existence. So the conclusion is that existence is the cause.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why is this method used? It is meant to establish the common cause by elimination. But what is the weakness? Think about what could go wrong.
The next lesson will address the weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية المثبتة للعلة المشتركة السبر وهو قسمة غير منحصرة كأن يقال مثلاً : علة كون السواد مرئياً إما وجوده أو كونه عرضاً أو محدثاً أو لوناً، أوكونه سواداً والكل باطل سوى الوجود والله سبحانه موجود فيصح رؤيته فإذا قيل: قد تكون العلة) المقتضية لصحة الرؤية في السواد ( أمراً آخر سوى هذه الأقسام (قيل) في الجواب ( لا دليل على ثبوت ذلك الأمر الآخر ( فينتفي ) وهذا رجوع إلى أول الطريقين وقد انكشف لك ضعفه . قوله : (السبر) في الصحاح سبرت الجرح أسبره إذا نظرت ما غوره .
The sixth observatory - the fifth aim: What are the strong methods? That which establishes the common cause: al-sabr (exploration), which is a non-exhaustive division, such as saying, for example: The cause of blackness being visible is either its existence, or its being an accident, or its being originated, or its being a color, or its being blackness, and all are false except existence, and Allah, exalted, exists, so His visibility is valid. If it is said: The cause necessitating the correctness of visibility in blackness might be another matter besides these divisions, it is said in answer: There is no proof for the existence of that other matter, so it is negated. And this is a return to the first of the two ways, and its weakness has been shown to you. His saying: (al-sabr) in al-Sihah: I explored the wound, I explore it, when I look at its depth.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the method of السبر (sabr), which is a method for identifying the common cause by division. It is described as a non-exhaustive division (قسمة غير منحصرة), meaning that the possibilities listed may not cover all alternatives. An example is given: the cause of blackness being visible is divided into several possibilities (existence, being an accident, being originated, being a color, being blackness), all of which are rejected except existence. Since God exists, He is visible. The weakness is exposed: the opponent can object that the cause might be something else not listed. The answer is that there is no proof for that other matter, so it is negated. However, the commentator notes that this is a return to the first way, which is weak. A lexical gloss explains the origin of the term 'sabr' from examining a wound.

Key Terms
سبر
قسمة غير منحصرة
علة مشتركة
رؤية
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers assume that the division in al-sabr is exhaustive, making the method conclusive.
Correction: The definition explicitly says it is 'non-exhaustive' (قسمة غير منحصرة), meaning the list may not cover all possibilities, so the method is inherently weak.

Checkpoint
What does 'non-exhaustive division' (قسمة غير منحصرة) mean in the definition of al-sabr?
A) The division covers all possible causes.
B) The division is limited to only one cause.
C) The list of possibilities may not include all alternatives.
D) The division is based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-sabr is a non-exhaustive division method that lists possible causes and eliminates them until one remains, but its incompleteness weakens its conclusiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author used a human example (blackness) to conclude about Allah. Have you ever applied a weak analogy to God? What does this tell you about the danger of relying on incomplete reasoning in your faith? How will you seek stronger proofs?
?
Al-sabr leaves the door open for doubt. Is there an area of your life where you have settled for a weak proof because it was comfortable? What would it cost you to face the uncertainty and seek a stronger foundation?

The Objection and Weakness of al-Sabr
Presents the objection to al-sabr, the weak response, and a lexical gloss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to al-sabr that the real cause may lie outside the listed possibilities, and why the standard response—requiring proof for the unlisted cause—is considered weak.

Core Idea
The weakness of sabr: the cause might be something not listed, and demanding proof for it is an inadequate answer.

This lesson completes our examination of al-sabr by presenting the objection that a critic might raise: the true cause could be something other than the listed possibilities. We will see the standard rebuttal and why it fails to salvage the method.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part starting with 'fidha qīla' (فإذا قيل). The opponent says: 'The cause might be another matter besides these divisions.'
This is a direct challenge to the completeness of the division.
2
Understand the response
The response is: 'There is no proof for the existence of that other matter, so it is negated.' This shifts the burden of proof to the objector.
This is a logical move but is considered weak because absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
3
Identify the weakness
The author then says: 'This is a return to the first of the two ways, and its weakness has been shown to you.' He refers to an earlier discussion (the first way) which was already shown to be weak.
4
Explore the lexical note
Read the linguistic note: 'sabr' originally means to probe a wound. This etymology explains why the method is called 'exploration'.
This is a helpful mnemonic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية المثبتة للعلة المشتركة السبر وهو قسمة غير منحصرة كأن يقال مثلاً : علة كون السواد مرئياً إما وجوده أو كونه عرضاً أو محدثاً أو لوناً، أوكونه سواداً والكل باطل سوى الوجود والله سبحانه موجود فيصح رؤيته فإذا قيل: قد تكون العلة) المقتضية لصحة الرؤية في السواد ( أمراً آخر سوى هذه الأقسام (قيل) في الجواب ( لا دليل على ثبوت ذلك الأمر الآخر ( فينتفي ) وهذا رجوع إلى أول الطريقين وقد انكشف لك ضعفه . قوله : (السبر) في الصحاح سبرت الجرح أسبره إذا نظرت ما غوره .
The sixth observatory - the fifth aim: What are the strong methods? That which establishes the common cause: al-sabr (exploration), which is a non-exhaustive division, such as saying, for example: The cause of blackness being visible is either its existence, or its being an accident, or its being originated, or its being a color, or its being blackness, and all are false except existence, and Allah, exalted, exists, so His visibility is valid. If it is said: The cause necessitating the correctness of visibility in blackness might be another matter besides these divisions, it is said in answer: There is no proof for the existence of that other matter, so it is negated. And this is a return to the first of the two ways, and its weakness has been shown to you. His saying: (al-sabr) in al-Sihah: I explored the wound, I explore it, when I look at its depth.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the method of السبر (sabr), which is a method for identifying the common cause by division. It is described as a non-exhaustive division (قسمة غير منحصرة), meaning that the possibilities listed may not cover all alternatives. An example is given: the cause of blackness being visible is divided into several possibilities (existence, being an accident, being originated, being a color, being blackness), all of which are rejected except existence. Since God exists, He is visible. The weakness is exposed: the opponent can object that the cause might be something else not listed. The answer is that there is no proof for that other matter, so it is negated. However, the commentator notes that this is a return to the first way, which is weak. A lexical gloss explains the origin of the term 'sabr' from examining a wound.

علة
قسمة غير منحصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that demanding proof for an unlisted cause is a strong rebuttal.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this response is weak, because it fails to address the possibility that the cause is simply not listed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the response to the objection considered weak?
A) Because the objector might be right.
B) Because it assumes the list is exhaustive.
C) Because it is a return to a previously shown weak method.
D) Because the response is based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-sabr is weak because its division may be incomplete, and the standard rebuttal—demanding proof for an unlisted cause—is insufficient, as it reverts to a previously flawed approach.

?
